0316212377|2.0|I have followed Mr. Banks faithfully since his first book and have never before found him quite so lacking in ideas and character development. Feels like he was on a tight deadline so a lot of the things he was developing in the book get truncated at very awkward, disconcerting or simply silly ways. This book, unlike his others, is almost not at all about humans (or non-AI aliens). It is all about the Minds (his notation for the uber-developed AI's that embody the ships of the Culture) and other AI-like entities. The problem with making it about the AIs is that it feels a lot like a cheat. They are all-seeing, all-powerful and when all else fails they cannot die since they just transmit their Mind elsewhere. Mr. Banks' naming convention around the Minds was an interesting construct for communicating how the Minds are so awesome they don't have to take themselves seriously. Unfortunately in this book he takes it way way farther than what is reasonable and you end up skipping pages and pages of 'email' headers with a long list of nonsensical or tongue-in-cheek names whose Minds then only appear for one or two sentences.<br /><br />The core (and really only) story line: a super advanced civilization has voted to be Sublimed, passing into the mysterious beyond where high-tech folks go when they are bored of the 'real'. No spoiler here that just days before this event that has been planned for 2 generations is to take place, it is suddenly put in jeopardy because it turns out their main religion was possibly a farce. Big ho-hum. The Minds involved ask themselves \\"why are we even getting involved?\\" (I echo the question, though my answer would have been different: 'don't'). Even the Gzilt in the know (the race about to be sublimed), also wonder if it is worth it to make any fuss about this since a large percent of the population already thought their religion was bogus anyway. In any case, this 'threat' to the sublimation leads to a cascade of bloody senseless killings that are accepted and non-intervened or revenged by the Culture since The Minds are only focused on finding the Truth, all morality and justice be damned. I kept waiting for one of the author's truly excellent human or humanoid characters that are able to bridge the guileless Minds and human spirit, creating compelling story lines. If not, then at least some plot twist so I could start caring. Maybe some end-of-the-universe risk. Nah. How could I care about a few trillion beings either staying in the Real and living the awesome carefree, scarcity-free life of an advanced civilization or going into some eternal spiritual paradise. 6 of one, half a dozen of the other. Hard to be stressed either way. And btw, they could have it both ways and some go and some stay.<br /><br />The one developed human character is basically taken along for a ride, and unlike other successful unwitting heroes, she never really rises above just being at best a silent passenger and at worst an anchor around the awesome avatars and Minds dealing with the situation. The author creates 2 characters that I thought had great potential and then bafflingly ignores, abandons of kills off without any further development. One is a really neat bodyguard android that has a flaw in his systems and thinks it is living in a simulation. I thought that was a promising premise. he does one heroic deed, then spends the rest of time asleep and finally get woken up to be killed off immediately. The second was one of the Minds, Caconym, one of the few which are actually more than just a silly name and a bunch of bold letters on the page, that gets a little bit of interesting character development at first and then completely disappears.<br /><br />There is a third character worth noting that would no doubt be in a tie with Jar Jar Binks as one of the silliest creations in sci-fi dom. It is a 'familiar' named Pyan. We never really understand if it is a pet, a tolerant reincarnated second cousin, a machine or what. Itis a small carpet-like thing whose sole addition to the story is to have 2-3 word non-sequiturs. Why is it there at all? why should we care?<br /><br />If you have never read a book by this author, do NOT start with this one, or you will probably never pick up another one and his earlier works are well-worth the read. [[ASIN:B007K4K8YC Surface Detail (Culture)]] is a great story that deals with deep and interesting issues like virtual slavery and torture. It is well-written and the Minds play a good role but are not the flawless excessively and annoyingly sarcastic Gods they play in this book.<br /><br />I look forward to Mr. Banks going back to writing books that will make me savor every page and sadden me when I reach the end rather than this one that made me skip pages and wonder when it would finally end.

B00887490Y|4.0|This is a raw look at a dying town that's been hit by a series of mysterious disappearances. Children have been vanishing practically out from under their parents' noses. We see the fear piling on top of the economic depression that has struck this town. There are the cars up on blocks in front yards full of junk - the suspicious, careful exchanges among the patrons of the local diner - and the general bitterness that's settling like volcanic ash over the town.<br /><br />After some twists and turns, this movie does provide a resolution to the mystery, albeit a rather improbable and partial one. Along the way, you're sometimes not sure if you're being projected into a dream or if you've just been taken to another level of stark reality.<br /><br />However, it's not the overall plot that's really important here. It's the atmosphere that draws you in, and individual harrowing scenes, such as the town nurse's grueling pursuit of the snatcher's van.<br /><br />Above all, I appreciated \\"Tall Man\\" for its courage in photographing women as they look in real life, rather than as the finished products of Hollywood make-up departments. B0030B0S2Q|5.0|In addition to the masterful acting and great performances, I really have to credit the writer(s) of this script with the important philisophical and societal statement they make about the future psychopathic behavior of the privatized corporation controlling deep-space exploration and operations.<br /><br />What we see portrayed in the film is really horrific. That a for profit company could master the science of manipulating human beings, duplicating them, and treating them like robots.  And making those robots think and feel they are human, only to violate, crush and shatter the existence of each and every one, on schedule, without end, from here to eternity.<br /><br />There is a seemingly endless list of the evil and psychopathic behavior the modern corporation is capable of and actively pursues and accomplishes.  Most of these were categorized neatly and convincingly in the documentary \\"The Corporation\\" in 2004.  But the scary and probable future evolution of this evil in society is chillingly and effectively portrayed in this fictional film.<br /><br />- a robot with an all too advanced human interaction (custom designed emoticon faces and voice tones, no less) specifically designed to manipulate a human so completely as to isolate them from the rest of humanity and micromanage them to a optimal production point from their birth to their death<br />- a mass produced human droid mix making individual REAL human beings irrelevant.  Why use real human beings, or give them any shot at these positions when robots are so much safer, cheaper, more consistent and of course, most importantly, more obedient and expendable?<br />- A completely manufactured life story and identity in a series of videos, and other stimuli, to hypnotize a human like being into thinking and feeling real when they are not.<br /><br />The level of psychopathy and evil described here and portrayed in the movie is so massive, so unthinkable, it is really quite stunning.<br />But at the point near the end of the film that you catch on that he's not really human, he's not really going home, this has all been an expertly manufactured charade, for the sake of production and profit, you feel spiritually violated, disgusted and horrified at the inhumanity corporations have made us collectively capable of.<br /><br />Folks, this is a scary and all too realistic future glimpse into our society, the way it is and the way it is headed with corporations running things with unchecked power.  If humans are irrelevant for space exploration and fuel missions, why stop there?  What's to say that what was going on down on earth during the same time period of that film wasn't even scarier?  What other professions, functions, geographical regions, etc. did corporations (or in our current tense WILL corporations) decide individual humanity doesn't or shouldn't have a role in and shove us aside?<br /><br />What if it's decided that more perfectly manipulated and manufactured humans are the only ones fit to serve in Congress, to be policemen, to be teachers?  And each one with an iphone in their pocket, and an earpiece talking to them with Gerty's voice, guiding them along the perfectly optimal corporate manipulated path to make their makers rich?<br /><br />This also brings to mind the movie \\"Surrogates\\" with Bruce Willis.  Gee, if we can all be perfect, better, and invincible, living through a robot who can be or look like anyone we want, why leave home, why leave bed?  Just let THEM do it all for us!<br /><br />Modern technology has accomplished some awesome things, but we are on the threshold with run-amok technology with no limits and no checks, of losing our humanity, our privacy, the quality of our lives, and genuine freedom.<br /><br />This film helped give an excellent glimpse into that scary future, and I hope among the other revered qualities of the film, this topic and that glimpse are discussed and taken seriously.<br /><br />Oh and on an artistic note, the musicscore by Clint Mansell is the perfect musical accompaniment to the insanity the main character is put through.  A seemingly progressive upbeat piano melody, reminiscent of hard work and progress, haunted and interrupted by occasional off key aberrations signifying the insanity of the situation, eventually overtaken by the spooky space tones, signifying the realization of being perfectly controlled, isolated and eventually discarded.  Bravo.

Maybe we should be more careful about what goes on the air!<br /><br />Back on earth we tend to believe what we see on TV, and a Star Trek Universe looks pretty inviting (except there are no bathrooms on Starships). We also tend to think that actors behave in life like their characters. This is sometimes a point of contention for movie stars who are trying to shop for groceries! \\"Galaxy Quest\\" explores these issues in a gentle but touching way.<br /><br />Here we look at the \\"reality\\" of a sci-fi fantasy universe. Only, it's just as unreal, but the characters have to take some of it seriously because they are stuck in it. Its like looking at the reflection of a mirror. Wonderful stuff!<br /><br />The characters, well, they're just so funny and sometimes so sweet. They could not have a better cast!<br /><br />The film actually does play with our emotions. Although it is a comedy, some of the characters die (not who we expect) and it is very touching and sad. Some characters are tortured, and that is painful to watch. It's because as silly as everyone is, we like them and feel for them. When the \\"chief engineer\\" gets the Beryllium Sphere working again, he calls his team for a group hug. How sweet is that?<br /><br />The script writing is very witty and clever.<br /><br />Imagine a universe where aliens can make technology but \\"Our attempts to use it have proved disastrous\\". I could say the same of my AT&T Go Phone. Come on, this is wildly silly and fun!<br /><br />The film pays homage to other films of the genre (The line - \\"Theremins from the Klaatu Nebula\\" is a reference to \\"The Day The Earth Stood Still\\" which used theremins (the musical instrument) in the score). It's all played tongue-in-cheek to great effect.<br /><br />And it proves that sci-fi doesn't have to take itself so seriously to be entertaining.<br /><br />Watch it for the jokes, they come fast and frequent.<br /><br />Some of the best lines from the film (I'm paraphrasing here):<br /><br />\\"I have ONE job to do on this ship. It's STUPID, but I'm going to do it!\\"<br /><br />\\"Listen to me -- it's all real!\\"<br /><br />\\"Did you even WATCH the show?\\"<br /><br />\\"Set it to Screen Saver Two\\"<br /><br />When negotiating with an enemy alien about a potential doomsday weapon:<br />\\"We don't know what it is either. (referring to the Omega 13 device) We were hoping you did.\\"<br /><br />Pros:<br /><br />Wonderful cast - top notch<br /><br />Good set design and props.<br /><br />Good special effects.<br /><br />Wonderful jokes.<br /><br />Excellent characters and interactions.<br /><br />Nice musical score.<br /><br />So silly it is absurd in an amusing way.<br /><br />Cons:<br /><br />None to speak of really,<br /><br />(I have it on VHS and it looks just fine.)clever.<br /><br />Imagine a universe where aliens can make technology but \\"Our attempts to use it have proved disastrous\\". Sometimes he appears to be unable to shed those New York mannerisms, inflections, which, while he remains one of the major actors of this generation, an audience viewer can spot a mile away.<br /><br />In his portrayal of Louis Cypher, all those mannerisms and traits, all those de Niro-isms, are gone. De Niro's character is bone-chilling, blood-curdling, and eerie. This was a part in which he apparently totally and completely immersed himself, both off and on camera. Alan Parker, the brilliant director of this film, has said that de Niro was so unapproachable and eerie on the set that he decided to just allow him to direct himself when the cameras started. De Niro himself says that his portrayal of Cypher was his imitation of Martin Scorcese. If true, that comment is enough to change one's opinion of Scorcese.<br /><br />This is not to say that the supporting cast doesn't measure up. On the contrary. B00979CS50|5.0|As a long time Neil Young (and Crazy Horse) fan, I have come to expect, even on his best albums, a mixed bag - for what makes him great (his willingness to cast absolutely everything aside for the sake of inspiration) is also what makes him frustrating (see Greendale, his last record with Crazy Horse...an album with considerable potential that came out sounding like a first draft in bad need of an edit).  With his newest, Psychedelic Pill, however, the inspiration comes across undaunted with very little of this aforementioned downside.  This is a passionate, positive album from a band that still sounds inspired and even hungry into their late 60s, and shows us, as Leonard Cohen did earlier this year, that getting old is entirely optional.<br /><br />Psychedelic Pill opens with \\"Driftin' Back\\", a long trance-jam meditation that never sounds flat or boring, and even feels somehow too short at 27 minutes.  The lyrics - which decry the sorry state of MP3s and consider the merits of a \\"Hip hop haircut,\\" whatever the hell that is - seem improvised throughout, but they are not the point here...the here-to-eternity instrumentals are, and they more than make up for any forced rhymes.  The title track, a short, celebratory stomper that's simple but not slight, follows, with frenetic speaker-to-speaker transitions that give it a fuzzy, hallucinogenic quality.  The third song, \\"Ramada Inn\\" is one of Neil's all-time greats, a simple paean to love over time with a few amazing (and deeply moving - almost devastating) chord changes.  The extended solos here are, again, never, ever boring, and sound as fresh, loud, and inspired as anything Neil has played in ages.  The first disk ends with \\"Born in Ontario\\" a joyous country-rock chugger that tells Neil's story much more succinctly - and perhaps effectively - than he did over 490 pages of his recent autobiography.<br /><br />Disc two is no let-up.  \\"Twisted Road\\" picks up, both lyrically and musically, where \\"Born in Ontario\\" leavesoff, celebrating the past without lamenting its passing, and then \\"She's Always Dancing\\", replete with more impassioned soloing, provides an answer to the bitter-sweetness of \\"Ramada Inn\\" by extolling the virtues of being in the moment, \\"Out on the edge,\\" where there are \\"No ties to bind [us] down.\\"  Next, \\"For the Love of Man\\" provides a simple, soft respite, but is by no means a throwaway, its lovely piano and ambling pace giving it the qualities of an old-time spiritual.<br /><br />The album ends with the excellent \\"Walk Like a Giant,\\" a song as epic lyrically as it is musically, and that's saying something.  (I first heard it on his recent tour, and told my wife and friend, five minutes in, \\"This is a damn great song.\\"  They both said they were about to say the same thing.)  There is lament here - \\"We were ready to save the world/ But the weather changed/ And the white got stained/ And it fell apart/ And it breaks my heart/ To think about how close we came/ I want to walk like a giant on the land\\" - but there is also, if only in the sheer, unceasing joyousness of the musical delivery, hope, as though Neil is saying that we didn't quite make it even while showing us that we still can.  \\"Walk Like a Giant\\" is a contender for my favorite Neil track of all time, and that is also really saying something.<br /><br />I've always maintained, when looking at Neil's recent work, one must judge him in relation to his 60s peers - in which case he almost always comes off better.  Psychedelic Pill, however, stands on its own merits as one of the best releases of 2012, no matter who you compare it to.  This album rocks in a way that is both thrilling and moving, and there are no let-downs, no moments where one might say, \\"If only he had done this...\\"<br /><br />And while there is no new ground broken here - Neil and the Horse employ the same simple, loud, repetitive structure that they always have - their old, hallowed ground is revisited with such gusto, such enthusiasm, thatthis makes no difference, and everything sounds new, fresh, and...well, groundbreaking.<br /><br />Psychedelic Pill is the work of a band not so much reclaiming what they always have been, but celebrating it.  And I am happy to be a part of that celebration.

When you are reading one of the Harry Potter books, you are transported to a different world--the writing is almost transparent, the medium (like floo powder) that simply takes you where you are supposed to go.  In this book, you are constantly aware of the author's voice, and the writing is often clunky and jarring (sometimes intentionally, I suspect).<br /><br />So, no, I didn't love it.  But there was still a lot to admire about the novel, which ultimately DID make me care about the characters (many of them, anyway).  And, in the end, I liked it.  I hope that in her next book for adults Rowling relaxes a little and lets the story unfold without so much embellishment that distracts from the story.

Very few castaways can claim to have survived so long at sea as Mr. Patel, and none in the company of an adult Bengal tiger.'<br /><br />\\"Yet, the story is hopeful and just awe-inspiring - Pi and Richard Parker are never friends, in the whole entire book. B003NFM3JU|4.0|To fully enjoy a movie the picture and sound are critical. This is especially true for Disney's, A Christmas Carol. I first saw this on IMAX in 3D and was very impressed. I later watched it on Netflix and enjoyed it, but the experience was not quite the same. While the story, animation, and voice acting were very good, much of the emotional impact of the story was lost at home without the engrossing effects of a six story three dimensional image and 10,000 watts of sound.<br /><br />However, I recently purchased a 3D TV and decided to pick up this 3D blu-ray. After watching it with the surround cranked and the glasses on I found the experience to be close to that of the theater. One drawback was that the aspect ratio was set at 2.40:1, which is the standard for widescreen cinema. As a result, the \\"black bars\\" were present at the top and bottom of my tv. Normally I wouldn't mind this, but the IMAX screen ratio is 1.43:1, and so to fit the full IMAX image into a 16:9 tv the black bars should have been present on the sides of the screen. As a result, there were times where I felt that I wasn't seeing everything that I should have been, similar to watching a widescreen film that has been cropped to fit a 4:3 TV. I'm not sure what the film's optimum aspect ratio was intended to be, but I'm inclined to think that 2.40:1 is not it.<br /><br />There are some original interpretations of the story and dialogue, and after reading some of A Christmas Carol, it seems to stay pretty true to the book. The chase scenes did get a little tiresome. I found myself wondering how much more screaming I could listen to, or how Jim Carrey managed to carry on like that in the studio. I think George C. Scott was a better Scrooge and other renditions took on a creepier interpretation that I thought worked better for the story, but this is a Disney film created in part for children, making it all the more appropriate.<br /><br />Overall I'm very happy with this purchase, especially on blu-ray 3D. The story is timeless, the voice acting is very good, the music is phenomenal, and the 3D effects are awesome. I think that the 3D is critical to fully enjoy this movie, as it doesn't quite hold up on the animation, voice-acting, and action alone. If the image filled the TV or was set at an IMAX ratio I would be inclined to give this 5/5.

1469984202|5.0|\\"WOOL: The Omnibus Edition\\" (Parts 1 through 5) by Hugh Howey<br /><br />4  out of 5<br /><br />*Be aware of possible spoilers*<br /><br />There is no shortage of post-apocalyptic dramas and adventures.  Nor is there a shortage in post-apocalyptic dramas involving life in a bunker or some other vast underground structure.  Hugh Howey's offering is to me a unique venture into this realm.<br /><br />WOOL 1 starts out with the highest authority of law in the silo, the sheriff, committing the highest offense in this world: wanting out.<br /><br />Part 1 is very depressing.  It tells the story of Holston and his dead wife Allison, the last to be sent out for cleaning.  Cleaning was the corporal punishment.  It meant death.  The outer world was desolate and toxic to life.  We have no idea why, only that for generations and generations it has been this way.<br /><br />Believed to be the last remaining humans on the planet are the few thousands that live below the ground in this vast structure.  The only view that these individuals living below ground have of the world is the video display in the upper cafeteria of the barren hill and dilapidated city outside of their world.<br /><br />This is what they go to clean.  As time goes on, the sensor arrays become foggy and opaque with the grime thrown around in the outside world.  People start to become weary as the view becomes grimy and soon a cleaner, a criminal, will need to be chosen.  Holston is the criminal in this case as Allison was years before.  But why?<br /><br />That is what part 1 explores.  Why would two intelligent, seemingly well-put-together individuals want to go out to clean and subsequently die?<br /><br />The story flashes to years earlier where the two began to uncover oddities in the fabric of reality they have always known.  Signs begin to point to a world that is not as bleak as everyone is apparently made to believe.  Eventually these discoveries drive Allison to the point of wanting out, to risk her life for an idea.  It was a decision that broke her husband causing him the same desire.<br /><br />WOOL is wholly depressing and simultaneously alluring.  The characters are all well-written which only tends to make the story even more bleak, more hopeless.  How do these people continue to survive in a landscape that would drive many of the steadiest of people to go mad?  Their survival is captivating.<br /><br />I wasn't immediately drawn wholly in, which is my criticism.  I wasn't overly surprised in part 1 either.  Maybe it was just me.  I pushed through the beginning as Hugh did make the story interesting enough to hold my curiosity and read forward despite the depressed nature of the dialogue.  Part 1's ending also didn't surprise me in the way I would have wanted.  It's me being picky.  I saw enough clues in the reading of part 1 to easily see what was going on.  Hugh may not have been trying to surprise anyone, rather point out the faade making my critique possibly an unfair one, but it is how I felt.<br /><br />The true page-turning of the story didn't start to happen for me until near midway in WOOL 2.  A new Sheriff is needed.  Enter: Juliette, the mayor and deputy sheriff's top pick to replace the deceased Holston.  Juliette is a greaser, the lowest on the social structure within the silo.  She's a mechanic in the lower levels.  It is Juliette that really gets things going as her nomination is not a welcomed choice by the increasingly suspicious IT group, and you soon find out that there is far more about the world that people are purposefully not letting out.<br /><br />WOOL 3 through 5 continue this journey with an explosive opening for 3.  Where WOOL 1 and 2 were interesting, but slow, WOOL 3, 4, and 5 were much quicker, more compelling and caused my wife a lot of headache as I found it very difficult to put the book down.  I am now fully vested in this saga with<br /><br />I would certainly recommend picking this one up.  And I definitely look forward to the next chapters.  Hugh Howey did well overall on this.<br /><br />The breakdown ratings (out of 5) per episode in the omnibus:<br /><br />WOOL 1: 3 <br />WOOL 2: 4<br />WOOL 3: 5<br />WOOL 4: 5<br />WOOL 5: 5

B0020TS5LU|3.0|A wealthy English industrialist invites a score of friends and relatives to a shoot at his country estate, during the course of which he manages to threaten and infuriate a number of them, by withdrawing support for their business plans, canceling the allowances of relatives and abusing nearly everyone, That night, he's murdered, and the following day a quirky detective shows up in order to sort things out, gather everyone into the same room, and identify the guilty party. The traditional English drawing-room murder mystery.<br /><br />Except when it's done by Robert Altman. Altman brings all his skills and quirks to what would otherwise be yet another overly affected episode of Masterpiece Theater, making it into something very different. You've got the gorgeous Altman photography, for one thing, with long, involved scene that move through house, turn corners, and have dozens of characters entering and exiting. The effect is one of being inside of a stage play, and when it works, it works very well. But you also have Altman's more annoying filmic tics to deal with. The mood of the movie moves from solemn to slapstick and back again, and you're never quite sure what you should be feeling. Everyone is quite tense following the murder, and who should show up to investigate, but a comically bumbling detective inspector played by Stephen Fry, accompanied by a suffering- but deadly serious- sergeant. In the end, we, the viewers, know who did it, and why, courtesy of a confession freely given to a total stranger, but the guests and the police never do find out what happened, at least by the end of the movie, so far as are allowed to see.<br /><br />What saves this film and makes it worth watching is the collection of outstanding performances by leading English actors in a wide range of roles. There's Maggie Smith as the vituperative old aunt, a role she really owns in modern cinema. Michael Gambon doing the old industrialist. Helen Mirren as Head of House. Alan Bates as Jennings, the tortured, alcoholic butler with a secret that eats at him. Jeremy Northam doing a fabulous job singing his way through his role as Ivor Novello. Frank Thornton, late of &#34;Are You Being Served?&#34; as Mr. Burkett. The list goes on and on, with one superb performance after another, the sole exception being Bob Balalban delivering yet another wooden, stereotypical performance.<br /><br />So why three stars? Well, as entertaining as it is at times, it just doesn't really hang together. At the end the viewer has the feeling that they've been flipping back and forth between two different versions of the same movie, one a drama and the other a farce. B002U6DVQM|3.0|I've had to admit this before, but I don't know much about the creator of 8 , Federico Fellini.  I've certainly heard of him for decades, but until recently, I had not seen any of his films.  I recently watched AMARCORD, and found it energetic and evocative, but kind of empty as well.  A man looking back on his childhood with clear memories of how he FELT, portraying events in the over-important manner that kids think about the things that happen to them...like nothing is more important.  I enjoyed the film, but didn't quite understand the fervor.<br /><br />8  caused many similar feelings for me.  It's filmed in gorgeous black and white, and it's even more of a dreamlike experience than AMARCORD.  On the surface, it tells the story of a famous Italian film director, Guido (played by the really terrific Marcello Mastroianni), who has perhaps had some sort of minor breakdown or creative collapse, and has gone to a spa somewhere in Italy.  Presumably, he has gone to get away from it all for a while, but he is surrounded by producers, critics, actors, who are all ready for him to start his next film.  It's clear Guido has no clear idea of what his film is about, except it is clearly meant to be autobiographical...yet he isn't sure what he's saying with the film.  Guido doesn't exactly have writer's block...he's got TOO much inspiration.  Seemingly, he desires to cram his entire life into the film, making it an homage to the important women in his life, and perhaps an apology.  While Guido clearly knows a lot of men...it is the women who have been important to him and who he has treated poorly throughout his life.  He remembers his mother, the village prostitute (very different than the village prostitute in AMARCORD), his many girlfriends, his wife, his sister, his current mistress.  They are all different.  He clearly has affection for them, and they mostly have some for him as well...but are frustrated, angry even tormented by his poor treatment.  They have offered him love, and he has given much less in return.<br /><br />The story is intermixed with numerous odd fantasies that Guido has.  He imagines scenes such as living in a harem with all his women, who are happy to share his attention.  He remembers scenes from his life.  Each piece he conjures is like a little absurdist piece...ridiculous and revealing.  But Fellini has even filmed his \\"normal\\" scenes like some crazy dance sequence...camera swirling, voices coming from all directions, extreme close-ups.  So even Guido's \\"real life\\" feels dreamlike.  The spa is an odd place, apparently real, yet somehow fantastical in its operation.  The movie set being built for Guido is a huge launching pad for a spaceship...yet we get absolutely no idea how a travel into space has anything to do with the movie Guido is apparently creating.<br /><br />8  explores (in my mind...I chose not to read any essays before throwing my thoughts out there) the crush of fame, as everyone around him is drowning Guido in their own demands.  It explores the psyche of a man who can't be faithful and/or can't return love.  It explores the creative process itself.  It's a film about a guy making a film about a guy who makes films.  Mastroianni is certainly a stand-in for Fellini himself...the film must surely by auto-biographical to some degree.  It feels too specific not to be.<br /><br />The cinematography is splendid.  The editing feels way ahead of its 1963 time.  The performances all nicely balance realism and an over-the-top cartoonishness...suitable for the circus-like atmosphere of the proceedings.  The music is appropriately loud and insistent.  We sense Guido is in a pressure cooker, and all these loud, sweaty people, loud, brassy music and swirling cameras are keeping him off balance and ready to explode.  Even in his calmest moments, and there are many, Guido is thinking, thinking, thinking.  How will he meet the demands of those around him...and why should he?<br /><br />The movie wasadmirable...yet for me it left me only bemused, not really entranced.  The character of Guido is simply too unlikeable.  He's charming as can be...but Guido (and thus, Fellini) is guilty of too much navel gazing and (as one character says near the end) assumes that people will find his life interesting.  Yes, Fellini is a beloved director and ground-breaking filmmaker...but exploring what a jerk he is and how tormented by his own fame is a bit of a bore.  I was unable to get through the film in one sitting...it took me three chunks of roughly 45 minutes to get to the end.  I feel dreadfully shallow saying this, especially about a film that is practically popping with energy and creativity.  But for me, the shallow philosophizing (really, just the director trying to excuse and ask forgiveness for piggish behavior) just doesn't make it compelling.<br /><br />I viewed the Criterion Blu-Ray.  The picture is splendidly crisp.  Dialogue, strangely, often seemed slightly out of synch with the actors.  Not sure if this isn't from the original.  But the subtitles are HORRIFIC.  I've complained about Criterion's subtitles before...they insist on white subtitles even on black & white films, with little or no outlining of the letters.  This has been a pain before, but 8  has many scenes with significant swaths of white, and I found myself having to pause from time to time just to squint and read the subtitles.  Criterion does such a great job with so many other aspects of what they do...why for goodness' sake can't they do better with the all-important subtitles?<br /><br />8  was worth seeing.  It's a classic and it's an important part of cinematic history...thus, I feel I've checked off a film on the list of \\"you can't be a true movie buff if you haven't seen these films.\\"  Yet it felt a little too much like homework for me to give it a ringing endorsement.

B007T8BYWE|5.0|If you've tried any of GabySoft's FlipPix Art series, you probably understand why these puzzles are so popular. The apps contain picture logic puzzles in which cells in a grid have to be colored or left blank according to numbers given at the side of the grid to reveal a hidden picture. Solving the FlipPix Art puzzles is a marvelously fun way to develop and practice logic skills. These kinds of puzzles are known as nonograms or griddlers, and they're extremely popular, with entire websites devoted to them. Each puzzle consists of an array of tiles, each of which is either filled or empty, Your goal is to determine which tiles are filled and which are empty.<br /><br />In this puzzle type, the numbers measure how many unbroken sets of filled-in tiles there are in any given row or column. For example, a clue of \\"2 4 1\\" would mean there are sets of two, four, and one filled tiles, in that order, with at least one blank tile between successive groups.<br /><br />If this isn't clear enough, don't worry, the FlipPix app includes an excellent tutorial to get you started. Puzzles come in three grid sizes:<br /><br />1. 5x5 grid. These are pretty easy, and they're great for beginners. There are up to three hints available, but you probably won't need them.<br /><br />2. 10x10 grid. These are much more challenging, and they're my favorite size. In a few of these, I had to use some of the hints.<br /><br />3. 15x15 grid. Brutal. I have yet to finish one of these without having to restart the puzzle a few times.<br /><br />For any grid size, you're only allowed two errors. On the third error, you have to restart the puzzle. In the Options menu, you can set the puzzles to ignore errors, but where's the challenge in that?<br /><br />FlipPix is one of those game concepts that would be clunky and awkward on paper, but plays beautifully on a computer or tablet. If you're not familiar with griddlers, give FlipPix a try, and I'm betting you'll be hooked on these puzzles.<br /><br />One tip for beginners: It's just as important to determine which tiles are empty as which tiles are filled. For example, if a row or column is a \\"1 1 1 1,\\" if you know the location of some of the filled tiles, before and after the filled tile will be an empty tile. Finding the empty tiles will help to find more filled tiles, and so on. It's an iterative process. Also, try to solve as much of the puzzle as you can before using the hints. If you use a hint early in the going, the two random tiles that are revealed may not help you very much. If you'll solve as much of the puzzle as you can before using a hint, the revealed tiles are much more likely to help you complete the puzzle.<br /><br />The model plane is a P-51D Mustang, one of the finest US fighter planes of World War II.<br /><br />The puzzles ran perfectly on my Kindle Fire HD.<br /><br />Note: The descriptions for the FlipPix puzzles indicate that the download is about half a megabyte. However, after the initial download, another download of about 10 MB is required, so make sure that you have that much storage space available.e tip for beginners: It's just as important to determine which tiles are empty as which tiles are filled. B006LSZECO|5.0|Gillian Flynn's GONE GIRL is a most important novel about America; she makes you think back to Bellow, Updike, and Didion in their primes.<br /><br />Disguised ingeniously and maliciously as a suspense thriller about a \\"sociopath,\\" GONE GIRL is the Profane Comedy of ever-more-mindless, wired-up America, no country for old (or young) loners in which being Original is a Sin and the already enormous, quickly growing Court Of Public Opinion, run by schlock media ever more expert at playing games primates play, is becoming the de facto law of the land. And even worse, the blaring herdspeak is becoming \\"reality.\\" As to this Vanity Fair, there is no sanity there.<br /><br />After inevitable, sour disillusionment has set in once the hopeless dream of Flawless Romance has invariably gone bad, Flynn's \\"sociopath\\" concocts a perfect spousal crime in that perp's all-too-plausible quest to impose a penal continuation of ideal marriage according to Hollywood's endless succession of \\"light romantic comedies\\" and the rest of the relentless and business-profitable love-dream huckstering in all the other familiar venues. It's a journey into the ghastly. You might have a dark dream in which Bram Stoker rewrites \\"the Bickersons.\\"<br /><br />Flynn is merciless in her disgust. Unspokenly, her savage, pitiless, resigned satire is an inspired riff on the Scott Peterson schlockfest of the previous decade, and like great jazz Flynn's story is immensely more insightful than, as well as different from, the original version. It becomes a Big Story.<br /><br />In Flynn's novel, the wife, Amy Elliott Dunne, disappears. Evidently she is pregnant. The husband, Nick Dunne, Must Have Done It; The Husband Is Always Guilty, isn't he? (Nick has a cleft in his chin: recall the old ditty, \\"Dimple on the chin, the Devil within\\"; the Devil has been \\"Old Nick\\" for centuries; \\"Dunne\\" = Done). Now in their thirties, both Nick and Amy have lost their writing jobs in the \\"futureland\\" of Manhattan after the Internet destroys the paper-publishing business in no more than a decade. Nick's sister, Go (Margo), also in Manhattan, has also lost her job, hers having been in the Financial Sector during the Great Recession. As the novel opens, the marital magic is long gone from the marriage of Nick and Amy as they live in a \\"bank-owned, recession-busted, price-reduced mansion\\" in a development of largely empty houses. Nick and Go run a tavern, The Bar, which they bought with the last of Amy's trust fund.<br /><br />The Perfect Crime in GONE GIRL is strictly contemporary. It entails a careful, complex scheme of entrapment of the other spouse sprung by attracting and masterfully manipulating the media to create a nationally devoured melodrama played out on millions of screens through superb, unerring novelistic stand-ins for the usual suspects--I can't help thinking here of Barbara Walters, Nancy Grace, Dateline, and on and on--in which that errant spouse becomes Guilty In The Court Of Public Opinion which in turn rouses the Homo sap primates en masse which in turn makes Local Law = Politics.<br /><br />GONE GIRL dramatizes not the \\"wisdom of crowds\\" but the stupidity of crowds.<br /><br />GONE GIRL is an enrapturing, dismaying and spot-on Comedy of Disgust, of Species Contempt, that is finally and necessarily a drama of self-loathing, the only possible escape being the Grand Cackle--sometimes enough to bring tears of laughter to your eyes--during Flynn's inspired satire and parody.<br /><br />Better yet, Flynn tells her story through her two major characters, the Dunnes, neither of whom is more than a little reliable, in a tour de force of modern verisimilitude; like such masters as Faulkner and Kurosawa in, respectively, LIGHT IN AUGUST and RASHOMON, the truth comes out through somewhat dubious or at least parochial and conflicting testimony and only with the active imagination of readers or viewers who must act as jurors of sorts, sifting and weighing the direct and implicit action of the novel as well as their own reactions, especially when the tale draws to a close and the comedy has become more bitter than sweet.<br /><br />Apropos: in GONE GIRL there finally seems not so much an ending as the reaching of an impasse in the Love Story, a dark State of Nature in the Gender Wars, the standing of \\"romance\\" on its head. Flynn couldn't be more up-to-date and prophetic in step with the primatologists of today who, along with others, are rather coldly reviewing human nature and \\"relationships.\\" In short, natural selection is hardly the perfect engineer, is it?<br /><br />The backdrop of GONE GIRL is drop-dead on. Flynn's wired-up America of today captures much in the Grand Surround: the Great Public has been relentlessly primed for exploitation by a comparatively small sector comprised top-to-bottom of utterly callous media schlockmeisters and their parasites such as \\"celebrity defense attorneys\\" and \\"personality journalists,\\" all of them empowered by the instantaneous ubiquity of TV and the Internet and trained by production types in turn trained by richly rewarded scientists and researchers who have demystified pop art by radically improving empirical understanding of how to manage Public Opinion: Potential concert pianists who became button pushers; no mere concert halls for them: All the world's a cage of super chimps with reliable reactions, a captive audience facing through the bars a stage upon which the schlocksters enact stupidly simpleminded dramas and then laugh all the way to the bank.<br /><br />The Ellen Abbott Show (might she be something of an amalgam of Nancy Grace and Greta Van Sustern?) begins the National Melodrama, attacking Nick, and the vast audience of super chimps jumps up and down through the instigated emotions: outrage, pity, anger, and so forth, but always within an instant of becoming converted groupies should the Guilty One Prove Innocent or Ask Forgiveness. Nick, condemned on all sides before entering any courtroom, hires celebrity lawyer Tanner Bolt. (Should you Google \\"Tanner Bolt,\\" you'll discover that it is a company specializing in anchors, which Tanner certainly does, e.g., anchors like Grace and Walters. Flynn is perhaps the first true Googler as a novelist when it comes to helping you track down allusions, understand wordplays, and similar: for example, who is \\"Madeleine Elster\\" and why does Amy refer to herself by that name?)<br /><br />To say much more here would be to commit the spoiler sin. Besides, once you've found out whodunnit and wha'happened, you'll find Flynn's fierce satirical energy underscored and thoroughly satisfying. Indeed, a second reading is most rewarding.

The backstory that game makers concoct to differentiate one or the other are largely irrelevant, but Call of Atlantis goes all in with a detailed backstory that takes quite a bit of time to get through, initially.<br /><br />Once you get into game play mode, it's a very nice looking, sharp board with great graphics, high quality background music and good special effects.  The $5 cost definitely went into making a game that at least looks as premium as its price tag implies.<br /><br />Unfortunately, it's let down by a few quirks that mar the experience.  The game's crashed on me twice now, both times in various parts of the introductory portion.  During game play, it's impossible to make more than one move at a time.  This may sound silly, but in my preferred Jewels-type game, you can keep making moves while other sets of three are blown off the board; this allows for faster gameplay and the ability to 'stack' matches one after another, creating some interesting and otherwise inaccessible matches as game play progresses.  Here, you have to wait until each match is cleared before input is restored.  Grid spacing is also not ideal for the sausage-fingered among us.  I had several instances of swiping items only to have them spin in place, mocking my very competence as a player.<br /><br />Perhaps the most troubling issue I've discovered so far is that there's no way to exit the program. I had to hit the home key, then go into task manager and kill the game.  It's also a bit of a memory hog, apparently.  While it only shows 33 MB of memory used when I go to close it, it must use more because upon exiting, my entire home screen is missing and has to re-load.  It shouldn't be an issue for most users but for those of us loaded to the gills on older hardware it may present a problem.<br /><br />Is it worth $5?  Not really.  The graphics are great and sound quality outstanding, but it's simply too buggy and gameplay too restricted to be my #1 go-to time waster in this genre. 0143117009|5.0|The book proceeds in a measured and even-handed way. It is by no means a diatribe or one-sided argument that ignores other sides of the story. In the initial example of the Bitterroot Valley in Montana the author shows a willingness to look at all sides of the situation. He has chosen a place in the United States that does not have many prospects for job growth whatever the politics happen to be. It has costly environmental problems due to past practices, particularly concerning the mining industry.<br /><br />My one criticism is that the book could have been more succinct, a little less didactic, but perhaps that is largely unavoidable given the author's stated purpose. A most interesting part of the book concerns those examples from history that show one society as a microcosm, analogous to what planet Earth has become for its inhabitants in this day and age. Questions naturally arise that are pertinent: How was it that the Easter Islanders cut down their last trees? What were the conditions that led the southeastern Polynesians living on three small islands to get to the point of too many people with too little resources?  How could the Anasazi of Chaco Canyon have averted collapse due climate change? What brought about the collapse of the Mayans who at one time numbered in the millions? How was it that the Vikings were so set in their ways and did not take a lesson from the Inuit in the face of climate change?<br /><br />On a personal level, it's not hard to see how development keeps subtracting open space, crowding out those free and unfettered places where one can just innocently take a walk. It also seems evident that the more people that crowd this globe, the fewer freedoms individuals will have. At least if we are to live peacefully, having more and more people means more and more rules. Implicit is the fact that there is a point beyond which crowding causes more contention over the inevitability of scarce resources. Yet, despite limits that keep staring usin the face, the reckless development and the exploding human population show little sign of moderating. The author states that he is cautiously optimistic. But the focus on economic expansion without regard for the future is now so strong that it seems only disasters can create the climate for changing the political will, which is now way too preoccupied with short term benefits.<br /><br />In the section he devotes to modern societies, his four examples include Rwanda, the island of the Dominican Republic and Haiti, China, and Australia. In these examples we can see how environmental problems can have dire consequences. In Rwanda, he makes the point that over population causing contention for scarce resources not ethnic hatred brought on genocide. Despite the political repression in the Dominican Republic, the dictator Balaguer recognized the urgent need to protect the country's forests and took drastic top-down action, differentiating his country from Haiti. China has at least taken some action to control their population. The economy of Australia suffers as a result of bad environmental decisions.

You can always turn ghosts off altogether, but the feature could still stand to be improved.<br /><br />Add options to select how many ghosts appear on screen (and maybe make them more transparent), and the ability to control the game with user-mapped keyboard presses (allowing for Wiimote/hard keyboard control) and icade style controllers, and I wouldn't have complaints-and I'd definitely play it more. As it stands, I'll keep at it, but I'm not sure for how long.

B008JMKN4Y|4.0|Beautiful Disaster by author Jamie McGuire is a contemporary Young Adult Romance set in Eastern University.  Although this is categorised as YA, don't let that deter you. It reads like an Adult Contemporary Romance. Essentially, this is a story of two young adults, who for many years have been trapped in the vortex of life, forced to live in the shadow of their families' expectations and type-casting. Until finding each other, neither believed they could be anyone different.<br /><br />As we meet Abby Abernathy, you see an intelligent, worldly wise, yet immature woman trying to reinvent herself. She's recently moved to Eastern, and on appearances, blends in with the other `cashmere-wearing' Freshmen. Abby, with her dark and infamous history,  feels as though she is finally walking somewhere without the whispers of presumed familiarity of her past. She has left behind chaos, attempting to find order and stability.<br /><br />Travis Maddox - even his name engenders a picture of arrogance, headstrong and `bad boy'. He is the tattoed, `hot' University Hero as well as the undisputed, self-confessed man-whore. Yet, when he meets Abby, he is suddenly relieved by her lack of adoration, and her complete rejection of his single-minded attention. He can see the potential of real friendship and possibly love, something he has never experienced with a girl before.<br /><br />Together, these two are messy or as the title suggests, a `beautiful disaster'.  It is the story of their growing friendship, their struggles with change and acceptance, with many funny situations and heart-felt moments. Travis is intelligent and driven to succeed, and as the story reveals, very devoted and somewhat overbearing. Abby's smart and determined, and with her intelligence and wit, breaks down the barriers that Travis has built in self-protection, allowing him to be `real'. What ensues will occasionally cause you to cringe, but often laugh and sometimes cry.<br /><br />This is not an easy book to read, yet it was extremely hard to put down. Incredibly well written, it is filled with angst, irresponsible teenagers, heart-ache, forgiveness and pain. Simply put, love is not portrayed as cutesy and sweet, but as hard, tough and full of complications and compromise. Perhaps - real?<br /><br />Beautiful Disaster is passionate, gritty, and a very compelling book. On first read, I was annoyed by the see-saw relationship between Abby and Travis, as well as the immaturity of most of the characters. Having said that, on second reading, I loved it. The characters are only teenagers after all, so should be forgiven for being immature and selfish behavior. I look forward to the long-awaited sequel Walking Disaster, a story told in Travis' POV.

1400052181|4.0|The story of HeLa by Rebecca Skloot (The Immortal Life of Henrietta Lacks) is a very interesting book that combines the personal story of a family with a discussion of the science underlying cell culture. The book describes many of the scientific advances that HeLa was associated with such as developing techniques for transferring cells around the country. In doing so, it highlights the importance of sterility in doing this (explaining how one cell culture can readily contaminate another). HeLa cells were also important for developing the hybridoma technique of fusing different cells, for the initial mapping of chromosomes, for developing the polio vaccine.<br /><br />Some of the things that doctors used to do with patient's tissues were rather shocking before the present day; not only performing experiments on them but also doing various procedures to people without their knowledge. In this context, HeLa cells featured very much in the development of the ideas of privacy and the notions of IRBs and so forth.<br /><br />Overall I felt that the book imparted a lot of useful scientific knowledge and I found it really amazing how this one cell line has made such a huge impact on biological research. Of course I was wondering if these types of studies could be done today -- and, of course, the answer is no.

B008FSCNTK|1.0|I have loved Diana Krall's work for many years and have always enjoyed her forays into new and different realms.  I looked forward eagerly to this album, but it is a heartbreaking disappointment.  Not even the arousing pulchritude of the photos on the cover and in the insert can compensate for the unfortunate noises contained here.  The arrangements are terrible, trashy honky-tonk, creepy almost-country, and dragging, lugubriously percussive dirges.  The overwhelming presence of guitar, twanging and plinking, eviscerates Ms. Krall's uncharacteristically thin and weak piano.  Her voice, which usually takes flight with vitality and playfulness, seems weighed down with the attempt to find some sort of profundity in songs that are redundant and trivial.  All artists are entitled, even obligated, to experiment and to stretch themselves outside of their comfort zones, but this particular experiment is a failure.  I hope Ms. Krall will return to the fertile fields she has cultivated so marvellously in the past.  Please, no more sad ghosts from a rundown turn-of-the- (19th) century bordello.

1451627297|5.0|This has been said many times; \\"Stephen King has done it again.\\" However, I think this truly is his crowning achievement. He took one of the most darkest events in the history of America and turned it into an epic journey combining actual events and people along with fictional events and characters. The story beings with the main character, Jake Epping (I love how the last name describes him. Epping meaning he goes on an Epic journey), a high school/GED teacher who's friend, Al Templeton owns a diner that has a time portal in it's pantry. The time portal transfers anyone who walks in there to September 9th, 1958. Al who is now sick and dying of lung cancer asks Jake to go back in time to prevent the Kennedy Assassination. After Al briefs him on how the time portal works and how to be careful in the past, Jake agrees to carry on the mission not only for his dying friend but for America itself. Not only does Jake, who now goes by the name George Amberson in the past try to prevent JFK's death, he also prevents other past dangers from happening while also making a new life for himself. On the book jacket of this book, it tells us that the story portrays time travel as more realistic. This book surely does. I won't go into detail on how because those who haven't read it yet need to read it for themselves. The things that I really like about this book is how King has actual people interact with fictional characters. I also like how detailed the book is. In other words; there's an explanation for everything. Probably my most favorite part of the book is the price. The price for saving a person's life. I won't give any spoilers away but I will say this; saving John F. Kennedy's life. Those who were living in 1963 remember how sad and unsafe they felt but, what would have happened to the country had he lived? I know that most people like to believe that the country would have been much better off but where's the real proof to that? Of course, no one has the answer to that question but just look at the possibilities. Look at what JFK did between the years of 1961 and 1963 and look at the future. Think; \\"How would he have done that differently?\\" Anyway, Stephen King's 11/22/63 is a masterpiece. Although he is known to being the Godfather of Horror, I believe that this is his true masterpiece! My only criticism of the book is this; why did it have to end? Was an ending really necessary?

0142418471|3.0|This was required reading for the GLBT unit in my YA Lit class, and when I saw it was in first-person present tense, I knew I would find it extremely difficult to read.  However, I ended up really getting into the gay Will Grayson's chapters, written by David Levithan.  That particular story really worked well with first-person present tense, something I can only say about 1% of all the first-person present books I've ever read or attempted to read.  That Will has such an authentic, angsty, distinct teen voice.  There were lots of funny lines and moments in the gay Will's chapters, like his commentary on the term LOL, the revelation that his friend Simon pleasures himself to video game characters including Warlocks, and his frenemy Maura's despairing poetry.<br /><br />I had a much harder time getting into the nerdy, straight Will's story, written by John Green.  The main reason, of course, was because of the distracting first-person present tense.  That is SUCH a non-intuitive, bizarre way to narrate a novel.  \\"I do this.  I do that.  I say this.  I say that.\\"  Some stories work very well with third-person present, if you know how to write it well and understand when it's effective.  But first-person present is just so awkward and distracting to read, with very few exceptions.  I think it works in certain kinds of memoirs, short pieces of fiction, and books narrated by extremely young characters, but for YA contemporary, it's so out of place.  (Don't even get me started on the oversaturation of first-person period in modern YA!)  Seeing it in print just makes me angry, and I normally stop reading when I see a book has that tense/POV combo.<br /><br />The straight Will didn't seem like as much of an active player in his own story, so dominated was he by his larger than life gay best friend Tiny.  His voice also didn't seem distinctive enough to merit being first-person (besides the fact that first-person is so predominant in modern YA, and thus seemingly often chosen out of a mistaken belief that it's expected).  The gay Will's story would've been interesting enough for an entire book, but I felt the straight Will's story didn't have enough of a hook or plot structure.  It was also very hard to imagine any high school in America putting on a play like Tiny's, even in an &uuml;ber-liberal area like Greenwich Village or Berkeley.<br /><br />I'm also not a fan of this YA trend of alternating narrators/POV characters.  If you're really into one character's story, it's frustrating to switch gears for a whole chapter and focus on someone you might not like as much.  By the time the book gets back to the other character, his story might not be as fresh in your mind.  I don't know what so many writers these days have against the tried and true third-person omniscient, and why so many modern YA writers are convinced their book NEEDS to be first-person present tense.<br /><br />I enjoyed the gay Will's chapters and would've preferred the entire book be from his perspective.  I just wish more of a hook and distinctive voice had been used for the straight Will's chapters.  I think it's safe to say I probably won't be interested in Mr. Green's other books, though I might be interested in some of Mr. Levithan's other books.n out of a mistaken belief that it's expected). B0054YHB36|5.0|Look, any casual or non-Mariah Carey fan wouldn't appreciate this album. The people who fall into those categories would be perturbed that not enough dance songs or up tempo songs are apart of this LP. But people have to realize that it's not always about those type of songs or sure-shot filler songs that pander to the public, but that it's about well written and produced music. Music Box was a peaceful and ethereal album that any true Mariah fan would enjoy.    \\"Hero\\" and \\"Dreamlover\\" got all the attention they deserved back in the day, and people to this day still love Hero as they should. Hero makes you want to achieve your goals and stop feeling sorry for yourself.    The title track, \\"Music Box\\" is priceless. That is my favorite song on the album, with beautiful double vocals by Mariah coupled with tender/peaceful background musical production. I could listen to that song anyday and when I want to feel peace. One of the best parts of the song was from the 3:12 to the 3:16 mark of the song with Mariah's charateristic high register and dreamy piano and keyboard production in the background. That song should have been a bigger hit, I love that song.    \\"Now That I Know\\" is an upbeat quintessential early 1990s production. You feel like you flashed back to 1993 when you listen to that song. Track 6 or \\"Never Forget You\\" could serve as a sentimental love song that could also easily take you back to 1993.    I don't care what anyone says, but Mariah murdered Track 7 \\"Without You\\" and I think it sounded better than the original and I mean that with no disrespect to the original artists (Badfinger) who sung the song. And the public responded by making it one of the hottest singles of 1994. To me that was one of Mariah's best songs she ever sung. I love how she drags the \\"I Can't Live Anymore\\" part for so long and doesn't want to let the note go just like a dog wouldn't want to let go of a raw T-bone steak.    Track 8, \\"Just to Hold You Once Again\\" was an underrated gem.I like the chorus in the background too.    Track 9, \\"I've Been Thinking About You\\" was another early 1990s quintessential production and a song one can still dance to in 2012. Don't front, you know doggone well you can jam to that song on the dance floor or in your car, I know I could. I have always liked at the :36 to :37 second marks, how she put emphasis on \\"Now, I WANT More\\". Meaning that she wants more than mere thoughts about a guy she knows, she wants a relationship dammit!    Track 10, \\"All I Ever Wanted\\" is my second favorite track on the album as that song is a cool way to end a great album. I don't know how many times around my house I have sung that song out loud. It's so peaceful and a quintessential Mariah ballad.    Walter Afanasieff as usual deserves credit too for the great success of this album.
